`` And there 's Melita Ross , '' go on Peg .
`` She 's get the same bonnet on she have last time I be in Carlisle church six year ago .
Some folk have the knack of make thing last .
But look at the style Mrs. Elmer Brewer wear , will yez ?
Yez would n't think her mother die in the poor-house , would yez , now ? ''
Poor Mrs . Brewer !
From the tip of her smart kid shoe to the dainty cluster of ostrich tip in her bonnet -- she be most immaculately and handsomely array ; but I venture to think she could have take small pleasure in her fashionable attire that even .
Some of the unregenerate , include Dan , be shake with suppressed laughter , but most of the people look a if they be afraid to smile , l their turn should come next .
`` There 's old Stephen Grant come in , '' exclaim Peg viciously , shake her floury fist at him , `` and look a if butter would n't melt in his mouth .
He may be an elder , but he 's a scoundrel just the same .
He set fire to his house to get the insurance and then blame ME for do it .
But I get even with him for it .
Oh , yes !
He know that , and so do I !
He , he ! ''
Peg chuckle quite fiendishly and Stephen Grant try to look a if nothing have be say .
`` Oh , will the minister never come ? ''
moan Felicity in my ear .
`` Surely she 'll have to stop then . ''
But the minister do not come and Peg have no intention of stopping .
`` There 's Maria Dean . ''
she resume .
`` I have n't see Maria for year .
I never call there for she never seem to have anything to eat in the house .
She be a Clayton and the Claytons never could cook .
Maria sorter look a if she 'd shrunk in the wash , now , do n't she ?
And there 's Douglas Nicholson .
His brother put rat poison in the family pancake .
Nice little trick that , be n't it ?
They say it be by mistake .
I hope it WAS a mistake .
His wife be all rig out in silk .
Yez would n't think to look at her she be marry in cotton -- and mighty thankful to get marry in anything , it 's my opinion .
There 's Timothy Patterson .
He 's the mean man alive -- meaner 'n Sam Kinnaird even .
Timothy pay his child five cent apiece to go without their supper , and then steal the cent out of their pocket after they 've go to bed .
It 's a fact .
And when his old father die he would n't let his wife put his best shirt on him .
He say his second best be plenty good to be bury in .
That 's another fact . ''
`` I ca n't stand much more of this , '' wail Felicity .
`` See here , Miss Bowen , you really ought n't to talk like that about people , '' expostulate Peter in a low tone , goad thereto , despite his awe of Peg , by Felicity 's anguish .
`` Bless you , boy , '' say Peg good-humouredly , `` the only difference between me and other folk be that I say these thing out loud and they just think them .
If I tell yez all the thing I know about the people in this congregation you 'd be amaze .
Have a peppermint ? ''
To our horror Peg produce a handful of peppermint lozenge from the pocket of her skirt and offer u one each .
We do not dare refuse but we each hold our lozenge very gingerly in our hand .
`` Eat them , '' command Peg rather fiercely .
`` Mother do n't allow u to eat candy in church , '' falter Felicity .
`` Well , I 've see just a fine lady a your ma give their child lozenges in church , '' say Peg loftily .
She put a peppermint in her own mouth and suck it with gusto .
We be relieve , for she do not talk during the process ; but our relief be of short duration .
A bevy of three very smartly dress young lady , sweep past our pew , start Peg off again .
`` Yez need n't be so stuck up , '' she say , loudly and derisively .
`` Yez be all of yez rock in a flour barrel .
And there 's old Henry Frewen , still above ground .
I call my parrot after him because their nose be exactly alike .
Look at Caroline Marr , will yez ?
That 's a woman who 'd like pretty well to get married , And there 's Alexander Marr .
He 's a real Christian , anyhow , and so 's his dog .
I can always size up what a man 's religion amount to by the kind of dog he keep .
Alexander Marr be a good man . ''
It be a relief to hear Peg speak well of somebody ; but that be the only exception she make .
`` Look at Dave Fraser strutting in , '' she go on .
`` That man have thank God so often that he be n't like other people that it 's come to be true .
He be n't !
And there 's Susan Frewen .
She 's jealous of everybody .
She 's even jealous of Old Man Rogers because he 's bury in the best spot in the graveyard .
Seth Erskine have the same look he be bear with .
They say the Lord make everybody but I believe the devil make all the Erskines . ''
`` She 's get bad all the time .
What WILL she say next ? ''
whispered poor Felicity .
But her martyrdom be over at last .
The minister appear in the pulpit and Peg subside into silence .
She fold her bare , floury arm over her breast and fasten her black eye on the young preacher .
Her behaviour for the next half-hour be decorum itself , save that when the minister pray that we might all be charitable in judgment Peg ejaculate `` Amen '' several time , loudly and forcibly , somewhat to the discomfiture of the Young man , to whom Peg be a stranger .
He open his eye , glance at our pew in a startled way , then collect himself and go on .
Peg listen to the sermon , silently and motionlessly , until Mr. Davidson be half through .
Then she suddenly get on her foot .
`` This be too dull for me , '' she exclaim .
`` I want something more exciting . ''
Mr. Davidson stop short and Peg march down the aisle in the midst of complete silence .
Half way down the aisle she turn around and face the minister .
`` There be so many hypocrite in this church that it be n't fit for decent people to come to , '' she say .
`` Rather than be such hypocrite a most of you be it would be good for you to go mile into the wood and commit suicide . ''
Wheeling about , she stride to the door .
Then she turn for a Parthian shot .
`` I 've felt kind of worried for God sometimes , see He have so much to attend to , '' she say , `` but I see I need n't be , so long 's there 's plenty of minister to tell Him what to do . ''
With that Peg shake the dust of Carlisle church from her foot .
Poor Mr. Davidson resume his discourse .
Old Elder Bayley , whose attention an earthquake could not have distract from the sermon , afterwards declare that it be an excellent and edify exhortation , but I doubt if anyone else in Carlisle church taste it much or gain much good therefrom .
Certainly we of the King household do not .
We could not even remember the text when we reach home .
Felicity be comfortless .
`` Mr. Davidson would be sure to think she belong to our family when she be in our pew , '' she say bitterly .
`` Oh , I feel a if I could never get over such a mortification !
Peter , I do wish you would n't go tell people they ought to go to church .
It 's all your fault that this happen . ''
`` Never mind , it will be a good story to tell sometime , '' remark the Story Girl with relish .
CHAPTER XXII .
THE YANKEE STORM In an August orchard six child and a grown-up be sit around the pulpit stone .
The grown-up be Miss Reade , who have be up to give the girl their music lesson and have consent to stay to tea , much to the rapture of the say girl , who continue to worship her with unabated and romantic ardour .
To u , over the golden grass , come the Story Girl , carry in her hand a single large poppy , like a blood-red chalice fill with the wine of August wizardry .
She proffer it to Miss Reade and , a the latter take it into her singularly slender , beautiful hand , I saw a ring on her third finger .
I notice it , because I have hear the girl say that Miss Reade never wear ring , not like them .
It be not a new ring ; it be handsome , but of an old-fashioned design and setting , with a glint of diamond about a central sapphire .
Later on , when Miss Reade have go , I ask the Story Girl if she have notice the ring .
She nod , but seem disincline to say more about it .
`` Look here , Sara , '' I say , `` there 's something about that ring -- something you know . ''
`` I tell you once there be a story grow but you would have to wait until it be fully grow , '' she answer .
`` Is Miss Reade go to marry anybody -- anybody we know ? ''
I persist .
`` Curiosity kill a cat , '' observe the Story Girl coolly .
`` Miss Reade have n't tell me that she be go to marry anybody .
You will find out all that be good for you to know in due time . ''
When the Story Girl put on grown-up air I do not like her so well , and I drop the subject with a dignity that seem to amuse her mightily .
She have be away for a week , visit cousin in Markdale , and she have come home with a new treasure-trove of story , most of which she have hear from the old sailor of Markdale Harbour .
She have promise that morning to tell u of `` the most tragic event that have ever be know on the north shore , '' and we now remind her of her promise .
`` Some call it the ` Yankee Storm , ' and others the ` American Gale , ' '' she begin , sit down by Miss Reade and beaming , because the latter put her arm around her waist .
`` It happen nearly forty year ago , in October of 1851 .
Old Mr. Coles at the Harbour tell me all about it .
He be a young man then and he say he can never forget that dreadful time .
You know in those day hundred of American fishing schooner use to come down to the Gulf every summer to fish mackerel .
On one beautiful Saturday night in this October of 1851 , more than one hundred of these vessel could be count from Markdale Capes .
By Monday night more than seventy of them have be destroy .
Those which have escape be mostly those which go into harbour Saturday night , to keep Sunday .
Mr. Coles say the rest stay outside and fished all day Sunday , same a through the week , and HE say the storm be a judgment on them for do it .
But he admit that even some of them get into harbour later on and escape , so it 's hard to know what to think .
But it be certain that on Sunday night there come up a sudden and terrible storm -- the bad , Mr. Coles say , that have ever be know on the north shore .
It last for two day and score of vessel be drive ashore and completely wreck .
The crew of most of the vessel that go ashore on the sand beach be save , but those that strike on the rock go to piece and all hand be lose .
For week after the storm the north shore be strew with the body of drowned men .
Think of it !
Many of them be unknown and unrecognizable , and they be bury in Markdale graveyard .
Mr. Coles say the schoolmaster who be in Markdale then write a poem on the storm and Mr. Coles recite the first two verse to me . ''
` Here be the fisher ' hillside graf , The church beside , the wood around , Below , the hollow moaning wave Where the poor fisherman be drown . '' '
A sudden tempest the blue welkin tore , The seaman toss and torn apart Rolled with the seaweed to the shore While landsman gaze with ache heart . '
`` Mr. Coles could n't remember any more of it .
But the sad of all the story of the Yankee Storm be the one about the Franklin Dexter .
The Franklin Dexter go ashore on the Markdale Capes and all on board perish , the Captain and three of his brother among them .
These four young men be the son of an old man who live in Portland , Maine , and when he hear what have happen he come right down to the Island to see if he could find their body .
They have all come ashore and have be bury in Markdale graveyard ; but he be determine to take them up and carry them home for burial .
He say he have promise their mother to take her boy home to her and he must do it .
So they be take up and put on board a sail vessel at Markdale Harbour to be take back to Maine , while the father himself go home on a passenger steamer .
The name of the sail vessel be the Seth Hall , and the captain 's name be Seth Hall , too .
Captain Hall be a dreadfully profane man and use to swear blood-curdling oath .
On the night he sail out of Markdale Harbour the old sailor warn him that a storm be brew and that it would catch him if he do not wait until it be over .
The captain have become very impatient because of several delay he have already meet with , and he be in a furious temper .
He swear a wicked oath that he would sail out of Markdale Harbour that night and ` God Almighty Himself should n't catch him . '
He do sail out of the harbour ; and the storm do catch him , and the Seth Hall go down with all hand , the dead and the living find a watery grave together .
So the poor old mother up in Maine never have her boy bring back to her after all .
Mr. Coles say it seem a if it be foreordain that they should not rest in a grave , but should lie beneath the wave until the day when the sea give up it dead . '' ''
` They sleep as well beneath that purple tide As others under turf , ' '' quote Miss Reade softly .
`` I be very thankful , '' she add , `` that I be not one of those whose dear one ` go down to the sea in ship . '
It seem to me that they have treble their share of this world 's heartache . ''
`` Uncle Stephen be a sailor and he be drown , '' say Felicity , `` and they say it break Grandmother King 's heart .
I do n't see why people ca n't be content on dry land . ''
Cecily 's tear have be drop on the autograph quilt square she be faithfully embroider .
She have be diligently collect name for it ever since the precede autumn and have a goodly number ; but Kitty Marr have one more and this be certainly a fly in Cecily 's ointment .
`` Besides , one I 've get be n't pay for -- Peg Bowen 's , '' she lament , `` and I do n't suppose it ever will be , for I 'll never dare to ask her for it . ''
`` I would n't put it on at all , '' say Felicity .
`` Oh , I do n't dare not to .
She 'd be sure to find out I do n't and then she 'd be very angry .
I wish I could get just one more name and then I 'd be content .
But I do n't know of a single person who have n't be ask already . ''
`` Except Mr. Campbell , '' say Dan .
`` Oh , of course nobody would ask Mr. Campbell .
We all know it would be of no use .
He do n't believe in mission at all -- in fact , he say he detest the very mention of mission -- and he never give one cent to them . ''
`` All the same , I think he ought to be ask , so that he would n't have the excuse that nobody DID ask him , '' declare Dan .
`` Do you really think so , Dan ? ''
ask Cecily earnestly .
`` Sure , '' say Dan , solemnly .
Dan like to tease even Cecily a wee bit now and then .
Cecily relapse into anxious thought , and care sit visibly on her brow for the rest of the day .
Next morning she come to me and say : `` Bev , would you like to go for a walk with me this afternoon ? ''
`` Of course , '' I reply .
`` Any particular where ? ''
`` I 'm go to see Mr. Campbell and ask him for his name for my square , '' say Cecily resolutely .
`` I do n't suppose it will do any good .
He would n't give anything to the library last summer , you remember , till the Story Girl tell him that story about his grandmother .
She wo n't go with me this time -- I do n't know why .
I ca n't tell a story and I 'm frightened to death just to think of go to him .
But I believe it be my duty ; and besides I would love to get a many name on my square a Kitty Marr have .
So if you 'll go with me we 'll go this afternoon .
I simply COULD N'T go alone . ''
CHAPTER XXIII .
A MISSIONARY HEROINE Accordingly , that afternoon we beard the lion in his den .
The road we take be a beautiful one , for we go `` cross lot , '' and we enjoy it , in spite of the fact that we do not expect the interview with Mr. Campbell to be a very pleasant one .
To be sure , he have be quite civil on the occasion of our last call upon him , but the Story Girl have be with u then and have beguile him into good-humour and generosity by the magic of her voice and personality .
We have no such ally now , and Mr. Campbell be know to be virulently oppose to mission in any shape or form .
`` I do n't know whether it would have be any good if I could have put on my good clothes , '' say Cecily , with a rueful glance at her print dress , which , though neat and clean , be undeniably fade and RATHER short and tight .
`` The Story Girl say it would , and I want to , but mother would n't let me .
She say it be all nonsense , and Mr. Campbell would never notice what I have on . ''
`` It 's my opinion that Mr. Campbell notice a good deal more than you 'd think for , '' I say sagely .
`` Well , I wish our call be over , '' sigh Cecily .
`` I ca n't tell you how I dread it . ''
`` Now , see here , Sis , '' I say cheerfully , `` let 's not think about it till we get there .
It 'll only spoil our walk and do no good .
Let 's just forget it and enjoy ourselves . ''
`` I 'll try , '' agree Cecily , `` but it 's ever so much easy to preach than to practise . ''
Our way lay first over a hill top , gallantly plume with golden rod , where cloud shadow drift over u like a gypsying crew .
Carlisle , in all it ripely tint length and breadth , lay below u , bask in the August sunshine , that spill over the brim of the valley to the far-off Markdale Harbour , cup in it harvest-golden hill .
Then come a little valley overgrown with the pale purple bloom of thistle and elusively haunt with their perfume .
You say that thistle have no perfume ?
Go you to a brook hollow where they grow some late summer twilight at dewfall ; and on the still air that rise suddenly to meet you will come a waft of faint , aromatic fragrance , wondrously sweet and evasive , the distillation of that despise thistle bloom .
Beyond this the path wound through a forest of fir , where a wood wind weave it murmurous spell and a wood brook dimple pellucidly among the shadow -- the dear , companionable , elfin shadow -- that lurk under the low grow bough .
Along the edge of that wind path grow bank of velvet green moss , star with cluster of pigeon berry .
Pigeon berry be not to be eat .
They be woolly , tasteless thing .
But they be to be look at in their glowing scarlet .
They be the jewel with which the forest of cone-bearers love to deck it brown breast .
Cecily gather some and pin them on hers , but they do not become her .
I think how witch the Story Girl 's brown curl would have look twin with those brilliant cluster .
Perhaps Cecily be think of it , too , for she presently say , `` Bev , do n't you think the Story Girl be change somehow ? ''
`` There be time -- just time -- when she seem to belong more among the grown-ups than among u , '' I say , reluctantly , `` especially when she put on her bridesmaid dress . ''
`` Well , she 's the old of u , and when you come to think of it , she 's fifteen , -- that 's almost grown-up , '' sigh Cecily .
Then she add , with sudden vehemence , `` I hate the thought of any of u grow up .
Felicity say she just longs to be grown-up , but I do n't , not a bit .
I wish I could just stay a little girl for ever -- and have you and Felix and all the others for playmate right along .
I do n't know how it be -- but whenever I think of be grown-up I seem to feel tired . ''
Something about Cecily 's speech -- or the wistful look that have creep into her sweet brown eye -- make me feel vaguely uncomfortable ; I be glad that we be at the end of our journey , with Mr. Campbell 's big house before u , and his dog sit gravely at the veranda step .
`` Oh , dear , '' say Cecily , with a shiver , `` I 'd be hop that dog would n't be around . ''
`` He never bite , '' I assure her .
`` Perhaps he do n't , but he always look a if he be go to , '' rejoin Cecily .
The dog continue to look , and , a we edge gingerly past him and up the veranda step , he turn his head and kept on look .
What with Mr. Campbell before u and the dog behind , Cecily be tremble with nervousness ; but perhaps it be as well that the dour brute be there , else I verily believe she would have turn and flee shamelessly when we hear step in the hall .
It be Mr. Campbell 's housekeeper who come to the door , however ; she usher u pleasantly into the sitting-room where Mr. Campbell be read .
He lay down his book with a slight frown and say nothing at all in response to our timid `` good afternoon . ''
But after we have sit for a few minute in wretched silence , wish ourselves a thousand mile away , he say , with a chuckle , `` Well , be it the school library again ? ''
Cecily have remark a we be come that what she dread most of all be introduce the subject ; but Mr. Campbell have give her a splendid opening , and she plunge wildly in at once , rattle her explanation off nervously with tremble voice and flushed cheek .
`` No , it 's our Mission Band autograph quilt , Mr. Campbell .
There be to be a many square in it a there be member in the Band .
Each one have a square and be collect name for it .
If you want to have your name on the quilt you pay five cent , and if you want to have it right in the round spot in the middle of the square you must pay ten cent .
Then when we have get all the name we can we will embroider them on the square .
The money be to go to the little girl our Band be support in Korea .
I hear that nobody have ask you , so I think perhaps you would give me your name for my square . ''
Mr. Campbell draw his black brows together in a scowl .
`` Stuff and nonsense ! ''
he exclaim angrily .
`` I do n't believe in Foreign Missions -- do n't believe in them at all .
I never give a cent to them . ''
`` Five cent be n't a very large sum , '' say Cecily earnestly .
Mr. Campbell 's scowl disappear and he laugh .
`` It would n't break me , '' he admit , `` but it 's the principle of the thing .
And a for that Mission Band of yours , if it be n't for the fun you get out of it , catch one of you belong .
You do n't really care a rap more for the heathen than I do . ''
`` Oh , we do , '' protest Cecily .
`` We do think of all the poor little child in Korea , and we like to think we be help them , if it 's ever so little .
We ARE in earnest , Mr. Campbell -- indeed we be . ''
`` Do n't believe it -- do n't believe a word of it , '' say Mr. Campbell impolitely .
`` You 'll do thing that be nice and interesting .
You 'll get up concert , and chase people about for autograph and give money your parent give you and that do n't cost you either time or labour .
But you would n't do anything you dislike for the heathen child -- you would n't make any real sacrifice for them -- catch you ! ''
`` Indeed we would , '' cry Cecily , forget her timidity in her zeal .
`` I just wish I have a chance to prove it to you . ''
`` You do , eh ?
Come , now , I 'll take you at your word .
I 'll test you .
Tomorrow be Communion Sunday and the church will be full of folk and they 'll all have their best clothes on .
If you go to church tomorrow in the very costume you have on at present , without tell anyone why you do so , until it be all over , I 'll give you -- why , I vow I 'll give you five dollar for that quilt of yours . ''
Poor Cecily !
To go to church in a faded print dress , with a shabby little old sun-hat and worn shoe !
It be very cruel of Mr. Campbell .
`` I -- I do n't think mother would let me , '' she falter .
Her tormentor smile grimly .
`` It 's not hard to find some excuse , '' he say sarcastically .
Cecily crimson and sit up face Mr. Campbell spunkily .
`` It 's NOT an excuse , '' she say .
`` If mother will let me go to church like this I 'll go .
But I 'll have to tell HER why , Mr. Campbell , because I 'm certain she 'd never let me if I do n't . ''
`` Oh , you can tell all your own family , '' say Mr. Campbell , `` but remember , none of them must tell it outside until Sunday be over .
If they do , I 'll be sure to find it out and then our bargain be off .
If I see you in church tomorrow , dress a you be now , I 'll give you my name and five dollar .
But I wo n't see you .
You 'll shrink when you 've have time to think it over . ''
`` I sha 'n ' t , '' say Cecily resolutely .
`` Well , we 'll see .
And now come out to the barn with me .
I 've get the pretty little drove of calf out there you ever saw .
I want you to see them . ''
Mr. Campbell take u all over his barn and be very affable .
He have beautiful horse , cow and sheep , and I enjoy see them .
I do n't think Cecily do , however .
She be very quiet and even Mr. Campbell 's handsome new span of dappled gray fail to arouse any enthusiasm in her .
She be already in bitter anticipation living over the martyrdom of the morrow .
On the way home she ask me seriously if I think Mr. Campbell would go to heaven when he die .
`` Of course he will , '' I say .
`` Is n't he a member of the church ? ''
`` Oh , yes , but I ca n't imagine him fit into heaven .
You know he be n't really fond of anything but live stock . ''
`` He 's fond of tease people , I guess , '' I respond .
`` Are you really go to church to-morrow in that dress , Sis ? ''
`` If mother 'll let me I 'll have to , '' say poor Cecily .
`` I wo n't let Mr. Campbell triumph over me .
And I DO want to have as many name a Kitty have .
And I DO want to help the poor little Korean child .
But it will be simply dreadful .
I do n't know whether I hope mother will or not . ''
I do not believe she would , but Aunt Janet sometimes could be depend on for the unexpected .
She laugh and tell Cecily she could please herself .
Felicity be in a rage over it , and declare SHE would n't go to church if Cecily go in such a rig .
Dan sarcastically inquire if all she go to church for be to show off her fine clothes and look at other people 's ; then they quarrel and do n't speak to each other for two day , much to Cecily 's distress .
I suspect poor Sis wish devoutly that it might rain the next day ; but it be gloriously fine .
We be all wait in the orchard for the Story Girl who have not begin to dress for church until Cecily and Felicity be ready .
Felicity be her prettiest in flower-trimmed hat , crisp muslin , float ribbon and trim black slipper .
Poor Cecily stand beside her mute and pale , in her faded school garb and heavy copper-toed boot .
But her face , if pale , be very determined .
Cecily , have put her hand to the plough , be not of those who turn back .
`` You do look just awful , '' say Felicity .
`` I do n't care -- I 'm go to sit in Uncle James ' pew .
I WO N'T sit with you .
There will be so many stranger there , and all the Markdale people , and what will they think of you ?
Some of them will never know the reason , either . ''
`` I wish the Story Girl would hurry , '' be all poor Cecily say .
`` We 're go to be late .
It would n't have be quite so hard if I could have get there before anyone and slip quietly into our pew . ''
`` Here she come at last , '' say Dan .
`` Why -- what 's she get on ? ''
The Story Girl join u with a quizzical smile on her face .
Dan whistle .
Cecily 's pale cheek flush with understanding and gratitude .
The Story Girl wear her school print dress and hat also , and be gloveless and heavy shod .
`` You 're not go to have to go through this all alone , Cecily , '' she say .
`` Oh , it wo n't be half so hard now , '' say Cecily , with a long breath of relief .
I fancy it be hard enough even then .
The Story Girl do not care a whit , but Cecily rather squirm under the curious glance that be cast at her .
She afterwards tell me that she really do not think she could have endure it if she have be alone .
Mr. Campbell meet u under the elm in the churchyard , with a twinkle in his eye .
`` Well , you do it , Miss , '' he say to Cecily , `` but you should have be alone .
That be what I mean .
I suppose you think you 've cheat me nicely . ''
`` No , she do n't , '' speak up the Story Girl undauntedly .
`` She be all dress and ready to come before she know I be go to dress the same way .
So she keep her bargain faithfully , Mr. Campbell , and I think you be cruel to make her do it . ''
`` You do , eh ?
Well , well , I hope you 'll forgive me .
I do n't think she 'd do it -- I be sure feminine vanity would win the day over missionary zeal .
It seem it do n't -- though how much be pure missionary zeal and how much just plain King spunk I 'm doubtful .
I 'll keep my promise , Miss . .
You shall have your five dollar , and mind you put my name in the round space .
No five-cent corner for me . ''
CHAPTER XXIV .
A TANTALIZING REVELATION `` I shall have something to tell you in the orchard this evening , '' say the Story Girl at breakfast one morning .
Her eye be very bright and excite .
She look a if she have not sleep a great deal .
She have spend the previous evening with Miss Reade and have not return until the rest of u be in bed .
Miss Reade have finish give music lesson and be go home in a few day .
Cecily and Felicity be in despair over this and mourn a those without comfort .
But the Story Girl , who have be even more devoted to Miss Reade than either of them , have not , a I notice , express any regret and seem to be very cheerful over the whole matter .
`` Why ca n't you tell it now ? ''
ask Felicity .
`` Because the evening be the nice time to tell thing in .
I only mention it now so that you would have something interest to look forward to all day . ''
`` Is it about Miss Reade ? ''
ask Cecily .
`` Never mind . ''
`` I 'll bet she 's go to be marry , '' I exclaim , remember the ring .
`` Is she ? ''
cry Felicity and Cecily together .
The Story Girl throw an annoyed glance at me .
She do not like to have her dramatic announcement forestall .
`` I do n't say that it be about Miss Reade or that it be n't .
You must just wait till the evening . ''

THE LOVE STORY OF THE AWKWARD MAN -LRB- Written by the Story Girl -RRB- Jasper Dale live alone in the old homestead which he have name Golden Milestone .

The Alice behind him could bear no more .
She give a little choking cry that betray her presence .

He saw her stand there , amid the languorous shadow of August , pale with feeling , wide-eyed , tremble .

`` How dare you ?
You have spy on me -- you have creep in and listen !
How dare you ?

My dream be dead .
It could not live when it be betray .
And it be all I have .

I know that I be ridiculous !
What of it ?
It never could have hurt you !
Why must you creep in like this to hear me and put me to shame ?

Is it such a strange thing that I should have a heart like other men ?
This will make sport for you !
I , who love you good than my life , good than any other man in the world can love you , will be a jest to you all your life .

`` Jasper !
Jasper ! ''
cried Alice , find her voice .
His anger hurt her with a pain she could not endure .

Nothing matter at all , save that he love her and be angry with her .
`` Do n't say such dreadful thing to me , '' she stammer , `` I do not mean to listen .
I could not help it .
I shall never laugh at you .
Oh , Jasper '' -- she look bravely at him and the fine soul of her shone through the flesh like an illuminating lamp -- `` I be glad that you love me !
and I be glad I chance to overhear you , since you would never have have the courage to tell me otherwise .
Glad -- glad !
Do you understand , Jasper ? ''
Jasper look at her with the eye of one who , look through pain , see rapture beyond .
`` Is it possible ? ''
he say , wonderingly .
`` Alice -- I be so much old than you -- and they call me the Awkward Man -- they say I be unlike other people '' -- `` You ARE unlike other people , '' she say softly , `` and that be why I love you .
I know now that I must have love you ever since I saw you . ''
`` I love you long before I saw you , '' say Jasper .
He come close to her and draw her into his arm , tenderly and reverently , all his shyness and awkwardness swallow up in the grace of his great happiness .

CHAPTER XXVIII .
THE PATH TO ARCADY October that year gather up all the spilled sunshine of the summer and clad herself in it a in a garment .
The Story Girl have ask u to try to make the last month together beautiful , and Nature second our effort , give u that most beautiful of beautiful thing -- a gracious and perfect moon of fall leaf .
There be not in all that vanish October one day that do not come in with auroral splendour and go out attend by a fair galaxy of even star -- not a day when there be not golden light in the wide pasture and purple haze in the ripened distance .
Never be anything so gorgeous a the maple tree that year .
Maples be tree that have primeval fire in their soul .
It glow out a little in their early youth , before the leaf open , in the redness and rosy-yellowness of their blossom , but in summer it be carefully hide under a demure , silver-lined greenness .
Then when autumn come , the maple give up try to be sober and flame out in all the barbaric splendour and gorgeousness of their real nature , make of the hill thing out of an Arabian Nights dream in the golden prime of good Haroun Alraschid .
You may never know what scarlet and crimson really be until you see them in their perfection on an October hillside , under the unfathomable blue of an autumn sky .
All the glow and radiance and joy at earth 's heart seem to have break loose in a splendid determination to express itself for once before the frost of winter chill her beating pulse .
It be the year 's carnival ere the dull Lenten day of leafless valley and penitential mist come .
The time of apple-picking have come around once more and we work joyously .
Uncle Blair pick apple with u , and between him and the Story Girl it be an October never to be forget .
`` Will you go far afield for a walk with me to-day ? ''
he say to her and me , one idle afternoon of opal sky , pied meadow and misty hill .

We like to be alone together that last month , to think the long , long thought of youth and talk about our future .
There have grow up between u that summer a bond of sympathy that do not exist between u and the others .
We be old than they -- the Story Girl be fifteen and I be nearly that ; and all at once it seem a if we be immeasurably old than the rest , and possess of dream and vision and forward-reaching hope which they could not possibly share or understand .
At time we be still child , still interested in childish thing .
But there come hour when we seem to our two self very grow up and old , and in those hour we talk our dream and vision and hope , vague and splendid , a all such be , over together , and so begin to build up , out of the rainbow fragment of our childhood 's companionship , that rare and beautiful friendship which be to last all our life , enrich and enstarring them .
For there be no bond more lasting than that form by the mutual confidence of that magic time when youth be slip from the sheath of childhood and begin to wonder what lie for it beyond those misty hill that bind the golden road .
`` Where be you go ? ''
ask the Story Girl .
`` To ` the wood that belt the gray hillside ' -- ay , and overflow beyond it into many a valley purple-folded in immemorial peace , '' answer Uncle Blair .
`` I have a fancy for one more ramble in Prince Edward Island wood before I leave Canada again .
But I would not go alone .
So come , you two gay youthful thing to whom all life be yet fair and good , and we will seek the path to Arcady .
There will be many little thing along our way to make u glad .
Joyful sound will ` come ring down the wind ; ' a wealth of gypsy gold will be ours for the gathering ; we will learn the potent , unutterable charm of a dim spruce wood and the grace of flexile mountain ash fringe a lonely glen ; we will tryst with the folk of fur and feather ; we 'll hearken to the music of gray old fir .
Come , and you 'll have a ramble and an afternoon that you will both remember all your life . ''
We do have it ; never have it remembrance fade ; that idyllic afternoon of rove in the old Carlisle wood with the Story Girl and Uncle Blair gleam in my book of year , a page of live beauty .
Yet it be but a few hour of simple pleasure ; we wander pathlessly through the sylvan calm of those dear place which seem that day to be full of a great friendliness ; Uncle Blair saunter along behind u , whistle softly ; sometimes he talk to himself ; we delight in those brief revery of his ; Uncle Blair be the only man I have ever know who could , when he so will , `` talk like a book , '' and do it without seem ridiculous ; perhaps it be because he have the knack of choose `` fit audience , though few , '' and the proper time to appeal to that audience .
We go across the field , intend to skirt the wood at the back of Uncle Alec 's farm and find a lane that cut through Uncle Roger 's wood ; but before we come to it we stumble on a sly , wind little path quite by accident -- if , indeed , there can be such a thing a accident in the wood , where I be tempt to think we be lead by the Good People along such of their fairy way a they have a mind for u to walk in .
`` Go to , let u explore this , '' say Uncle Blair .
`` It always drag terribly at my heart to go past a wood lane if I can make any excuse at all for traverse it : for it be the by-ways that lead to the heart of the wood and we must follow them if we would know the forest and be know of it .
When we can really feel it wild heart beat against ours it subtle life will steal into our vein and make u it own for ever , so that no matter where we go or how wide we wander in the noisy way of city or over the lone way of the sea , we shall yet be draw back to the forest to find our most enduring kinship . ''
`` I always feel so SATISFIED in the wood , '' say the Story Girl dreamily , a we turn in under the low-swinging fir bough .
`` Trees seem such friendly thing . ''
`` They be the most friendly thing in God 's good creation , '' say Uncle Blair emphatically .
`` And it be so easy to live with them .
To hold converse with pine , to whisper secret with the poplar , to listen to the tale of old romance that beech have to tell , to walk in eloquent silence with self-contained fir , be to learn what real companionship be .
Besides , tree be the same all over the world .
A beech tree on the slope of the Pyrenees be just what a beech tree here in these Carlisle wood be ; and there use to be an old pine hereabouts whose twin brother I be well acquaint with in a dell among the Apennines .
Listen to those squirrel , will you , chatter over yonder .
Did you ever hear such a fuss over nothing ?
Squirrels be the gossip and busybody of the wood ; they have n't learn the fine reserve of it other denizen .
But after all , there be a certain shrill friendliness in their greeting . ''
`` They seem to be scold u , '' I say , laugh .
`` Oh , they be not half such scold a they sound , '' answer Uncle Blair gaily .
`` If they would but ` tak a thought and mend ' their shrew-like way they would be dear , lovable creature enough . ''
`` If I have to be an animal I think I 'd like to be a squirrel , '' say the Story Girl .
`` It must be next best thing to fly . ''
`` Just see what a spring that fellow give , '' laugh Uncle Blair .
`` And now listen to his song of triumph !
I suppose that chasm he clear seem a wide and deep to him a Niagara Gorge would to u if we leap over it .
Well , the wood people be a happy folk and very well satisfied with themselves . ''
Those who have follow a dim , wind , balsamic path to the unexpected hollow where a wood-spring lie have find the rarest secret the forest can reveal .
Such be our good fortune that day .
At the end of our path we find it , under the pine , a crystal-clear thing with lip unkissed by so much a a stray sunbeam .
`` It be easy to dream that this be one of the haunted spring of old romance , '' say Uncle Blair . ''
'T be an enchanted spot this , I be very sure , and we should go softly , speak low , l we disturb the rest of a white , wet naiad , or break some spell that have cost long year of mystic weaving . ''
`` It 's so easy to believe thing in the wood , '' say the Story Girl , shape a cup from a bit of golden-brown birch bark and fill it at the spring .
`` Drink a toast in that water , Sara , '' say Uncle Blair .
`` There 's not a doubt that it have some potent quality of magic in it and the wish you wish over it will come true . ''
The Story Girl lift her golden-hued flagon to her red lip .
Her hazel eye laugh at u over the brim .
`` Here 's to our future , '' she cry , `` I wish that every day of our life may be good than the one that go before . ''
`` An extravagant wish -- a very wish of youth , '' comment Uncle Blair , `` and yet in spite of it extravagance , a wish that will come true if you be true to yourselves .
In that case , every day WILL be good than all that go before -- but there will be many day , dear lad and lass , when you will not believe it . ''
We do not understand him , but we know Uncle Blair never explain his meaning .
When ask it he be wont to answer with a smile , `` Some day you 'll grow to it .
Wait for that . ''
So we address ourselves to follow the brook that steal away from the spring in it winding and doubling and tricky surprise .
`` A brook , '' quoth Uncle Blair , `` be the most changeful , bewitching , lovable thing in the world .
It be never in the same mind or mood two minute .
Here it be sigh and murmur a if it heart be break .
But listen -- yonder by the birch it be laugh a if it be enjoy some capital joke all by itself . ''
It be indeed a changeful brook ; here it would make a pool , dark and brooding and still , where we bend to look at our mirrored face ; then it grow communicative and gossip shallowly over a broken pebble bed where there be a diamond dance of sunbeam and no troutling or minnow could glide through without be see .
Sometimes it bank be high and steep , hung with slender ash and birch ; again they be mere , low margin , green with delicate moss , shelve out of the wood .
Once it come to a little precipice and fling itself over undauntedly in an indignation of foam , gather itself up rather dizzily among the mossy stone below .
It be some time before it get over it vexation ; it go boil and mutter along , fight with the rotten log that lie across it , and make far more fuss than be necessary over every root that interfere with it .
We be get tire of it ill-humour and talk of leave it , when it suddenly grow sweet-tempered again , swoop around a curve -- and presto , we be in fairyland .
It be a little dell far in the heart of the wood .
A row of birch fringe the brook , and each birch seem more exquisitely graceful and golden than her sister .
The wood recede from it on every hand , leave it lie in a pool of amber sunshine .
The yellow tree be mirror in the placid stream , with now and then a leaf fall on the water , mayhap to drift away and be use , a Uncle Blair suggest , by some adventurous wood sprite who have it in mind to fare forth to some far-off , legendary region where all the brook run into the sea .
`` Oh , what a lovely place ! ''
I exclaim , look around me with delight .
`` A spell of eternity be weave over it , surely , '' murmur Uncle Blair .
`` Winter may not touch it , or spring ever revisit it .
It should be like this for ever . ''
`` Let u never come here again , '' say the Story Girl softly , `` never , no matter how often we may be in Carlisle .
Then we will never see it change or different .
We can always remember it just a we see it now , and it will be like this for ever for u . ''
`` I 'm go to sketch it , '' say Uncle Blair .
While he sketch it the Story Girl and I sit on the bank of the brook and she tell me the story of the Sighing Reed .
It be a very simple little story , that of the slender brown reed which grow by the forest pool and always be sad and sighing because it could not utter music like the brook and the bird and the wind .
All the bright , beautiful thing around it mock it and laugh at it for it folly .
Who would ever look for music in it , a plain , brown , unbeautiful thing ?
But one day a youth come through the wood ; he be as beautiful a the spring ; he cut the brown reed and fashion it accord to his liking ; and then he put it to his lip and breathe on it ; and , oh , the music that float through the forest !
It be so entrance that everything -- brook and bird and wind -- grow silent to listen to it .
Never have anything so lovely be hear ; it be the music that have for so long be shut up in the soul of the sigh reed and be set free at last through it pain and suffering .
I have hear the Story Girl tell many a more dramatic tale ; but that one stand out for me in memory above them all , partly , perhaps , because of the spot in which she tell it , partly because it be the last one I be to hear her tell for many year -- the last one she be ever to tell me on the golden road .
When Uncle Blair have finish his sketch the shaft of sunshine be turn crimson and grow more and more remote ; the early autumn twilight be fall over the wood .
We leave our dell , say good-bye to it for ever , a the Story Girl have suggest , and we go slowly homeward through the fir wood , where a haunting , indescribable odour steal out to meet u .
`` There be magic in the scent of die fir , '' Uncle Blair be say aloud to himself , a if forgetting he be not quite alone .
`` It get into our blood like some rare , subtly-compounded wine , and thrill u with unutterable sweetness , a of recollection from some other fair life , live in some happy star .
Compared to it , all other scent seem heavy and earth-born , lure to the valley instead of the height .
But the tang of the fir summons onward and upward to some ` far-off , divine event ' -- some spiritual peak of attainment whence we shall see with unfaltering , unclouded vision the spire of some aerial City Beautiful , or the fulfilment of some fair , fadeless land of promise . ''
He be silent for a moment , then add in a low tone , `` Felicity , you love the scent of die fir .
If you be here tonight with me -- Felicity -- Felicity ! ''
Something in his voice make me suddenly sad .
I be comfort when I felt the Story Girl slip her hand into mine .
So we walk out of the wood into the autumn dusk .
We be in a little valley .
Half-way up the opposite slope a brush fire be burn clearly and steadily in a maple grove .
There be something indescribably allure in that fire , glow so redly against the dark background of forest and twilit hill .
`` Let u go to it , '' cry Uncle Blair , gaily , cast aside his sorrowful mood and catch our hand .
`` A wood fire at night have a fascination not to be resist by those of mortal race .
Hasten -- we must not lose time . ''
`` Oh , it will burn a long time yet , '' I gasp , for Uncle Blair be whisk u up the hill at a merciless rate .
`` You ca n't be sure .
It may have be light by some good , honest farmer-man , bent on tidy up his sugar orchard , but it may also , for anything we know , have be kindle by no earthly woodman a a beacon or summons to the tribe of fairyland , and may vanish away if we tarry . ''
It do not vanish and presently we find ourselves in the grove .
It be very beautiful ; the fire burn with a clear , steady glow and a soft crackle ; the long arcade beneath the tree be illuminate with a rosy radiance , beyond which lurk company of gray and purple shadow .
Everything be very still and dreamy and remote .
`` It be impossible that out there , just over the hill , lie a village of men , where tame household lamp be shin , '' say Uncle Blair .
`` I feel a if we must be thousand of mile away from everything we 've ever know , '' murmur the Story Girl .
`` So you be ! ''
say Uncle Blair emphatically .
`` You 're back in the youth of the race -- back in the beguilement of the young world .
Everything be in this hour -- the beauty of classic myth , the primal charm of the silent and the open , the lure of mystery .
Why , it 's a time and place when and where everything might come true -- when the men in green might creep out to join hand and dance around the fire , or dryad steal from their tree to warm their white limb , grow chilly in October frost , by the blaze .
I would n't be much surprised if we should see something of the kind .
Is n't that the flash of an ivory shoulder through yonder gloom ?
And do n't you see a queer little elfin face peer at u around that twisted gray trunk ?
But one ca n't be sure .
Mortal eyesight be too slow and clumsy a thing to match against the flicker of a pixy-litten fire . ''
Hand in hand we wander through that enchanted place , seek the folk of elf-land , `` and hear their mystic voice call , from fairy knoll and haunt hill . ''
Not till the fire die down into ash do we leave the grove .
Then we find that the full moon be gleam lustrously from a cloudless sky across the valley .
Between u and her stretch up a tall pine , wondrously straight and slender and branchless to it very top , where it overflow in a crest of dark bough against the silvery splendour behind it .
Beyond , the hill farm be lie in a suave , white radiance .
`` Does n't it seem a long , long time to you since we leave home this afternoon ? ''
ask the Story Girl .
`` And yet it be only a few hour . ''
Only a few hour -- true ; yet such hour be worth a cycle of common year untouched by the glory and the dream .
CHAPTER XXIX .
WE LOSE A FRIEND Our beautiful October be mar by one day of black tragedy -- the day Paddy die .
For Paddy , after seven year of a happy a life a ever a cat live , die suddenly -- of poison , a be suppose .
Where he have wander in the darkness to meet his doom we do not know , but in the frosty dawnlight he drag himself home to die .
We find him lie on the doorstep when we get up , and it do not need Aunt Janet 's curt announcement , or Uncle Blair 's reluctant shake of the head , to tell u that there be no chance of our pet recover this time .
We felt that nothing could be do .
Lard and sulphur on his paw would be of no use , nor would any visit to Peg Bowen avail .
We stand around in mournful silence ; the Story Girl sit down on the step and take poor Paddy upon her lap .
`` I s ` pose there 's no use even in pray now , '' say Cecily desperately .

Felicity and Cecily and Sara Ray cry so much that Aunt Janet lose patience completely and tell them sharply that they would have something to cry for some day -- which do not seem to comfort them much .

We leave the orchard , a sober little band , with the wind of the gray twilight blow round u .

And we hat Uncle Roger .

CHAPTER XXX .
PROPHECIES `` Here 's a letter for you from father , '' say Felix , toss it to me a he come through the orchard gate .

This letter be brief enough , but it be freight with a message of weighty import .

` Tai n't .

We go , Cecily still a little disappointed .
In late year I often wonder why the Story Girl refuse to tell her fortune that night .
Did some strange gleam of foreknowledge fall for a moment across her mirth-making ?
Did she realize in a flash of prescience that there be no earthly future for our sweet Cecily ?
Not for her be to be the lengthening shadow or the fading garland .
The end be to come while the rainbow still sparkle on her wine of life , ere a single petal have fall from her rise of joy .
Long life be before all the others who trysted that night in the old homestead orchard ; but Cecily 's maiden foot be never to leave the golden road .
CHAPTER XXXI .
THE LAST NUMBER OF OUR MAGAZINE EDITORIAL It be with heartfelt regret that we take up our pen to announce that this will be the last number of Our Magazine .
We have edit ten number of it and it have be successful beyond our expectation .
It have to be discontinue by reason of circumstance over which we have no control and not because we have lose interest in it .
Everybody have do his or her best for Our Magazine .
Prince Edward Island expect everyone to do his and her duty and everyone do it .
Mr. Dan King conduct the etiquette department in a way worthy of the Family Guide itself .
He be especially entitle to commendation because he labour under the disadvantage of have to furnish most of the question as well a the answer .
Miss Felicity King have edit our helpful household department very ably , and Miss Cecily King 's fashion note be always up to date .
The personal column be well look after by Miss Sara Stanley and the story page have be a marked success under the able management of Mr. Peter Craig , to whose original story in this issue , `` The Battle of the Partridge Eggs , '' we would call especial attention .
The Exciting Adventure series have also be very popular .
And now , in closing , we bid farewell to our staff and thank them one and all for their help and co-operation in the past year .
We have enjoy our work and we trust that they have too .
We wish them all happiness and success in year to come , and we hope that the recollection of Our Magazine will not be hold least dear among the memory of their childhood .

THE BATTLE OF THE PARTRIDGE EGGS Once upon a time there live about half a mile from a forrest a farmer and his wife and his son and daughter and a granddaughter .

The fox tell her he know where there be a partridges nest and a bluejays nest full of egg .

_BOOK_TITLE_ : Nathaniel_Hawthorne___The_Vision_of_the_Fountain_ ( From_ '' Twice_Told_Tales '' ) .txt.out
At fifteen , I become a resident in a country village , more than a hundred mile from home .
The morning after my arrival -- a September morning , but warm and bright a any in July -- I ramble into a wood of oak , with a few walnut-trees intermixed , form the close shade above my head .
The ground be rocky , uneven , overgrown with bush and clump of young sapling , and traverse only by cattle-paths .
The track , which I chance to follow , lead me to a crystal spring , with a border of grass , a freshly green a on May morning , and overshadow by the limb of a great oak .
One solitary sunbeam find it way down , and play like a goldfish in the water .
From my childhood , I have love to gaze into a spring .
The water fill a circular basin , small but deep , and set round with stone , some of which be cover with slimy moss , the others naked , and of variegated hue , reddish , white , and brown .
The bottom be cover with coarse sand , which sparkle in the lonely sunbeam , and seem to illuminate the spring with an unborrowed light .
In one spot , the gush of the water violently agitate the sand , but without obscure the fountain , or break the glassiness of it surface .
It appear a if some living creature be about to emerge -- the Naiad of the spring , perhaps -- in the shape of a beautiful young woman , with a gown of filmy water-moss , a belt of rainbow-drops , and a cold , pure , passionless countenance .
How would the beholder shiver , pleasantly , yet fearfully , to see her sit on one of the stone , paddle her white foot in the ripple , and throw up water , to sparkle in the sun !
Wherever she lay her hand on grass and flower , they would immediately be moist , a with morning dew .
Then would she set about her labor , like a careful housewife , to clear the fountain of withered leaf , and bit of slimy wood , and old acorn from the oak above , and grain of corn leave by cattle in drinking , till the bright sand , in the bright water , be like a treasury of diamond .
But , should the intruder approach too near , he would find only the drop of a summer shower glisten about the spot where he have see her .
Reclining on the border of grass , where the dewy goddess should have be , I bend forward , and a pair of eye meet mine within the watery mirror .
They be the reflection of my own .
I look again , and lo !
another face , deep in the fountain than my own image , more distinct in all the feature , yet faint a thought .
The vision have the aspect of a fair young girl , with lock of paly gold .
A mirthful expression laugh in the eye and dimple over the whole shadowy countenance , till it seem just what a fountain would be , if , while dance merrily into the sunshine , it should assume the shape of woman .
Through the dim rosiness of the cheek , I could see the brown leaf , the slimy twig , the acorn , and the sparkling sand .
The solitary sunbeam be diffuse among the golden hair , which melt into it faint brightness , and become a glory round that head so beautiful !
My description can give no idea how suddenly the fountain be thus tenant , and how soon it be leave desolate .
I breathe ; and there be the face !
I hold my breath ; and it be go !
Had it pass away , or fade into nothing ?
I doubt whether it have ever be .
My sweet reader , what a dreamy and delicious hour do I spend , where that vision find and leave me !
For a long time I sit perfectly still , wait till it should reappear , and fearful that the slight motion , or even the flutter of my breath , might frighten it away .
Thus have I often start from a pleasant dream , and then keep quiet , in hope to wile it back .
Deep be my musing , a to the race and attribute of that ethereal be .
Had I create her ?
Was she the daughter of my fancy , akin to those strange shape which peep under the lid of child 's eye ?
And do her beauty gladden me , for that one moment , and then die ?
Or be she a water-nymph within the fountain , or fairy , or woodland goddess peeping over my shoulder , or the ghost of some forsaken maid , who have drown herself for love ?
Or , in good truth , have a lovely girl , with a warm heart , and lip that would bear pressure , steal softly behind me , and thrown her image into the spring ?
I watch and wait , but no vision come again .
I depart , but with a spell upon me , which draw me back , that same afternoon , to the haunt spring .
There be the water gushing , the sand sparkling , and the sunbeam glimmering .
There the vision be not , but only a great frog , the hermit of that solitude , who immediately withdraw his speckle snout and make himself invisible , all except a pair of long leg , beneath a stone .
Methought he have a devilish look !
I could have slay him !
Thus do the Vision leave me ; and many a doleful day succeed to the parting moment .
By the spring , and in the wood , and on the hill , and through the village ; at dewy sunrise , burn noon , and at that magic hour of sunset , when she have vanish from my sight , I seek her , but in vain .
Weeks come and go , month roll away , and she appear not in them .
I impart my mystery to none , but wander to and fro , or sit in solitude , like one that have catch a glimpse of heaven , and could take no more joy on earth .
I withdraw into an inner world , where my thought live and breathe , and the Vision in the midst of them .
Without intend it , I become at once the author and hero of a romance , conjure up rival , imagine event , the action of others and my own , and experience every change of passion , till jealousy and despair have their end in bliss .
O , have I the burn fancy of my early youth , with manhood 's colder gift , the power of expression , your heart , sweet lady , should flutter at my tale !
In the middle of January , I be summon home .
The day before my departure , visit the spot which have be hallow by the Vision , I find that the spring have a frozen bosom , and nothing but the snow and a glare of winter sunshine , on the hill of the rainbow .
`` Let me hope , '' think I , `` or my heart will be as icy a the fountain , and the whole world as desolate a this snowy hill . ''
Most of the day be spend in prepare for the journey , which be to commence at four o'clock the next morning .
About an hour after supper , when all be in readiness , I descend from my chamber to the sitting-room , to take leave of the old clergyman and his family , with whom I have be an inmate .
A gust of wind blew out my lamp a I pass through the entry .
According to their invariable custom , so pleasant a one when the fire blaze cheerfully , the family be sit in the parlor , with no other light than what come from the hearth .
As the good clergyman 's scanty stipend compel him to use all sort of economy , the foundation of his fire be always a large heap of tan , or ground bark , which would smoulder away , from morning till night , with a dull warmth and no flame .
This even the heap of tan be newly put on , and surmount with three stick of red-oak , full of moisture , and a few piece of dry pine , that have not yet kindle .
There be no light , except the little that come sullenly from two half-burned brand , without even glimmer on the andiron .
But I know the position of the old minister 's arm-chair , and also where his wife sit , with her knitting-work , and how to avoid his two daughter , one a stout country lass , and the other a consumptive girl .
Groping through the gloom , I find my own place next to that of the son , a learned collegian , who have come home to keep school in the village during the winter vacation .
I notice that there be less room than usual , to-night , between the collegian 's chair and mine .
As people be always taciturn in the dark , not a word be say for some time after my entrance .
Nothing break the stillness but the regular click of the matron 's knitting-needles .
At time , the fire throw out a brief and dusky gleam , which twinkle on the old man 's glass , and hover doubtfully round our circle , but be far too faint to portray the individual who compose it .
Were we not like ghost ?
Dreamy a the scene be , might it not be a type of the mode in which depart people , who have know and love each other here , would hold communion in eternity ?
We be aware of each other 's presence , not by sight , nor sound , nor touch , but by an inward consciousness .
Would it not be so among the dead ?
The silence be interrupt by the consumptive daughter , address a remark to some one in the circle , whom she call Rachel .
Her tremulous and decayed accent be answer by a single word , but in a voice that make me start , and bend towards the spot whence it have proceed .
Had I ever hear that sweet , low tone ?
If not , why do it rouse up so many old recollection , or mockery of such , the shadow of thing familiar , yet unknown , and fill my mind with confused image of her feature who have speak , though bury in the gloom of the parlor ?
Whom have my heart recognize , that it throb so ?
I listen , to catch her gentle breathing , and strive , by the intensity of my gaze , to picture forth a shape where none be visible .
Suddenly , the dry pine caught ; the fire blaze up with a ruddy glow ; and where the darkness have be , there be she , -- the Vision of the Fountain !
A spirit of radiance only , she have vanish with the rainbow , and appear again in the firelight , perhaps to flicker with the blaze , and be go .
Yet , her cheek be rosy and life-like , and her feature , in the bright warmth of the room , be even sweet and tender than my recollection of them .
She know me !
The mirthful expression that have laugh in her eye and dimple over her countenance , when I behold her faint beauty in the fountain , be laugh and dimple there now .
One moment our glance mingle , -- the next , down roll the heap of tan upon the kindle wood , -- and darkness snatch away that Daughter of the Light , and give her back to me no more !
Fair lady , there be nothing more to tell .
Must the simple mystery be reveal , then , that Rachel be the daughter of the village squire , and have leave home for a boarding-school , the morning after I arrive , and return the day before my departure ?
If I transform her to an angel , it be what every youthful lover do for his mistress .
Therein consist the essence of my story .

CHAPTER I HAPPY JACK DROPS A NUT Save a little every day , And for the future put away .
Happy Jack .
Happy Jack Squirrel sit on the tip of one of the high branch of a big hickory tree .
Happy Jack be up very early that morning .
In fact , jolly , round , red Mr. Sun be still in his bed behind the Purple Hills when Happy Jack hop briskly out of bed .
He wash himself thoroughly and be ready for business by the time Mr. Sun begin his climb up in the blue , blue sky .

CHAPTER XI STRIPED CHIPMUNK 'S THANKSGIVING DINNER There 's nothing quite so sweet in life As make up and end strife .
Happy Jack .
Striped Chipmunk jump out of bed very early Thanksgiving morning .

`` Thanksgiving come but once a year , But when it come it bring good cheer .
For in my storehouse on this day Are pile of good thing hide away .
Each day I 've work from early morn To gather acorn , nut , and corn , Till now I 've plenty and to spare Without a worry or a care .
So light of heart the whole day long , I 'll sing a glad Thanksgiving song . ''
Promptly at the dinner hour Happy Jack appear come from one direction , and Chatterer the Red Squirrel come from another direction .

Then they stop and scowl .

They just have to smile in spite of themselves , and when Striped Chipmunk urge them to sit down and help themselves , they do .

`` It 's quite a family party , be n't it ? ''
say Striped Chipmunk , for you know they be all cousin .

When everybody have eat and eat until they could n't hold another thing , and it be time to think of go home , Striped Chipmunk insist that Happy Jack and Chatterer should divide between them the big , fat hickory nut that be leave , and they do without once quarrel about it .
`` Thanksgiving come but once a year , And when it come it bring good cheer , '' say Striped Chipmunk to himself a he watch his guest depart .
CHAPTER XII HAPPY JACK DOES SOME THINKING To call another a thief do n't make him one .
Happy Jack .
Happy Jack sit up in a chestnut tree , and his face be very sober .

`` There certainly must have be enough of those nut for all of u , and if I have n't be so greedy we might all have have a share .

`` Have you just find that out ?

CHAPTER XIII HAPPY JACK GETS A WARNING It matter not how smart you be , So be it you be heedless too .
It be n't what you know that count So much a what it be to you .
Happy Jack .
A fat Gray Squirrel be very tempting to a number of people in the Green Forest , particularly in winter , when get a living be hard work .

`` Hello , Tommy Tit !
What ail you ? ''

CHAPTER XIV .
HAPPY JACK 'S RUN FOR LIFE A coward he who run away When he should stay and fight , But wise be he who know when he Should run with all his might .
Happy Jack .
It be n't cowardly to run away when it be quite useless to stay and fight .
So it be n't so cowardly of Happy Jack Squirrel to turn tail and run the instant he catch sight of Shadow the Weasel .
No , Sir , it be n't cowardly at all , although it might have look so to you have you be there to see , for Happy Jack be big than Shadow .

Shadow be too quick for him , and though Happy Jack be ever so brave , he would have no chance at all in a fight with Shadow .

Poor Happy Jack !

CHAPTER XV WHO SAVED HAPPY JACK SQUIRREL ?

Happy Jack .

CHAPTER XIX TOMMY TIT AND HAPPY JACK PAY A VISIT As grow the mighty elm tree , From just a tiny seed , So often great thing happen From just a kindly deed .
Happy Jack .

Now you may not think that it be anything very great that Happy Jack should be on his way to Farmer Brown 's house .

that 's nothing ! ''
